Qira’ah Has No Substitute
Clarification that qira’ah has no substitute when unable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that qira’ah (recitation) has no substitute when one is unable, and explain the rulings for the illiterate (al-ummi) and the mute (al-akhras).

Core Idea
Recitation has no substitute when unable; the illiterate must still move lips.

In the previous lessons, you learned that standing, bowing, and prostrating have substitutes when one is unable to perform them due to illness or disability. But what about the recitation of the Qur’an? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq clarifies that qira’ah stands alone: it has no substitute. Even a patient who cannot recite prays without it. However, the text also addresses two special cases: the illiterate person who does not know the Qur’an, and the mute person who cannot speak. There is a difference of opinion on whether they must move their lips and tongue. Let us unpack the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The author only lists substitutes for standing, bowing, and prostrating. This implies that qira’ah has no substitute. So when a person is unable to recite due to illness or disability, they pray without recitation; the prayer is still valid.
This is the relied-upon Hanafi position.
2
Understand the illiterate and mute cases
For a person who cannot recite (al-ummi) or cannot speak (al-akhras), there are two views: (1) it is obligatory to move the lips and tongue (like the talbiyah in Hajj), and (2) it is not obligatory. The stronger view is the first, as it preserves the form of recitation.
Moving lips is a symbolic act to fulfill the spirit of the pillar.
3
Note the exception for limited knowledge
If the person only knows the phrase 'Al-ḥamdu lillāh', they say it once per rak'ah and do not repeat it. This contrasts with tashahhud, which can be repeated to fill the duration of the sitting because the sitting has a fixed time (the length of tashahhud).
The repetition is not the same; it is to occupy the time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واقتصار المصنف على بيان البدل للأركان الثلاثة أعني القيام والركوع والسجود إشارة إلى أن القراءة لا بدل لها عند العجز عنها فيصلي بغير القراءة وفي المجتبى : قيل في الأمي والأخرس يجب تحريك الشفة واللسان كتلبية الحج ، وقيل لا يجب وإذا لم يعرف إلا قوله «الحمد الله» يأتي به في كل ركعة ولا يكررها بخلاف التحيات في التشهد فإنه يكررها قدر التشهد لكون القعود مقدراً ا هـ.
And the author’s restriction to explaining the substitute for the three pillars, namely standing, bowing, and prostrating, indicates that recitation (qirā’ah) has no substitute when unable; so he prays without recitation. And in al-Mujtabā: It was said concerning the illiterate and the deaf-mute: moving the lips and tongue is obligatory like the talbiyah of Hajj, and it was said: not obligatory. And if he only knows how to say “Al-ḥamdu lillāh”, he says it in every rak‘ah and does not repeat it, unlike the tashahhud, for he repeats it to the extent of the tashahhud because the sitting is determined (in length). End quote.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement clarifying the author's (al-Marghinani) approach in the matn (Kanz al-Daqa’iq). By only specifying substitutes for standing, bowing, and prostrating, he implies that qira’ah (Qur’an recitation) has no substitute when one is unable to recite. Therefore, a patient who cannot recite prays without it. The text then cites al-Mujtaba regarding the illiterate (al-ummi) and the deaf-mute (al-akhras): there is disagreement on whether they must move their lips and tongue (like the talbiyah in Hajj) or not. If the person only knows the phrase “Al-ḥamdu lillāh”, he says it once per rak‘ah and does not repeat it, unlike the tashahhud which is repeated to fill the duration of the sitting because the sitting is measured (by the time needed for tashahhud).

Key Terms
الأمي (al-ummī): A person who cannot recite the Qur’an or does not know it.
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is unable to speak (mute).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) That these are the only important pillars.
B) That recitation has no substitute when one is unable.
C) That the sick person must always recite even if unable.
D) That the substitute for recitation is moving the head.

Key Takeaway
Recitation (qira’ah) has no substitute; when unable, pray without it, but those who can move lips should do so.

Falling of Conditions When Unable: Priority over Pillars
Reasoning that conditions fall more readily than pillars when unable, with examples of qibla and purity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi a fortiori (bil-awlā) principle to determine that conditions of prayer are excused when impossible, and state that no repetition is required upon recovery.

Core Idea
Conditions fall more readily than pillars: if pillars are waived due to inability, conditions are waived a fortiori.

This passage demonstrates a key Hanafi reasoning method: the a fortiori argument (bil-awlā). It applies to situations where a patient cannot fulfill certain conditions of prayer, such as facing the qibla or being free from impurity. The text argues that since even pillars (like standing, bowing, prostration) are excused when impossible, conditions are excused even more, and no repetition is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? A patient who cannot face the qibla prays facing another direction. The ruling: the prayer is valid and no repetition (i‘ādah) is required after recovery.
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
The proof is the a fortiori reasoning (bil-awlā) mentioned in the text: if pillars of prayer are dropped due to incapacity, then conditions are dropped even more because they are lesser in importance.
This is a rational proof based on the hierarchy of prayer elements.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (indication)
The text states: 'inability to fulfill conditions is not greater than inability to fulfill pillars.' Since pillars are waived without repetition, conditions are waived even more appropriately.
The logical link is that incapacity for the lesser element justifies its omission even more than for the greater element.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (disagreement)
Some other schools might require repetition if the patient could have been helped by someone else but did not ask. The Hanafi school (Abū Ḥanīfah) holds that ability through another is not true ability, so the prayer is still valid.
This is a key intra-school nuance.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih (preference)
The relied-upon (muftā bihī) position is that the patient prays as he can and does not repeat. This is based on the principle of ease and the a fortiori argument.
The text cites al-Badāʾiʿ and al-Khulāṣah as sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأشار بسقوط الأركان عند العجز إلى سقوط الشرائط عند العجز عنها بالأولى، فلو كان وجه المريض إلى غير القبلة ولم يقدر على التحويل إليها بنفسه ولا بغيره يصلي كذلك لأنه ليس في وسعه إلا ذلك ولا إعادة عليه بعد البرء في ظاهر الجواب لأن العجز عن تحصيل الشرائط لا يكون فوق العجز عن تحصيل الأركان وثمة لا تجب الإعادة فههنا أولى . في البدائع. وفي الخلاصة : فإن وجد أحداً يحوله فلم يأمره وصلى إلى غير القبلة جاز عند أبي حنيفة بناء على أن الاستطاعة بقوة الغير ليست بثابتة عنده وعلى هذا لو صلى على فراش نجس ووجد أحداً يحوله إلى مكان طاهر ثم قال مريض مجروح تحته ثياب نجسة، إن كان بحال لا يبسط تحته شيء إلا تنجس من ساعته له أن يصلي على حاله، وكذا لو لم يتنجس الثاني إلا أنه يزداد مرضه له أن يصلي فيه ا هـ.
And he (the author) indicated by the falling of the pillars due to incapacity that the conditions fall due to incapacity a fortiori (bil-awlā). So if the patient’s face is towards other than the qibla and he cannot turn towards it by himself or by someone else, he prays as he is, because that is all he can do, and there is no repetition (i‘ādah) upon recovery in the apparent answer (zāhir al-jawāb), because inability to fulfill conditions is not greater than inability to fulfill pillars, and there (i.e., for pillars) repetition is not required, so here it is even more appropriate. In al-Badā’i‘. And in al-Khulāṣah: If he found someone to turn him but did not order him and prayed towards other than the qibla, it is permitted according to Abū Ḥanīfah, based on the principle that ability through another’s strength is not considered established (lā thābitah) in his view. And upon this, if he prayed on an impure (najis) mat and found someone to move him to a pure place, then he (the author) said: A wounded patient under whom are impure clothes – if it is such that nothing can be spread under him without becoming impure immediately, he may pray as he is. Similarly, if the second (cloth) does not become impure but his illness would increase, he may pray in it. End quote.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof using the principle of a fortiori (bil-awlā). The author reasons that if the pillars of prayer (standing, bowing, prostration) are excused when impossible, then conditions such as facing qibla and ritual purity are excused even more readily, because conditions are generally considered lesser in importance. The text gives examples: if a patient cannot face qibla and cannot be turned, he prays as is and does not repeat the prayer after recovery, as stated in al-Badā’i‘. Al-Khulāṣah adds a nuance: if he could have been turned by someone else but did not ask, Abū Ḥanīfah still permits the prayer because ability through another is not considered true ability. Similarly, if the patient cannot avoid impurity due to his condition (e.g., a wound that contaminates anything), he prays in that state. The key point is that the inability to fulfill conditions is not greater than the inability to fulfill pillars, so if pillars are waived, conditions are waived even more so.

Key Terms
ظاهر الجواب (zāhir al-jawāb): The apparent answer, meaning the authoritative or well-known position in the school.
بالأولى (bil-awlā): A fortiori; a logical argument that if a stronger case (here, pillars) is excused, then a weaker case (conditions) is even more excused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the patient not have to repeat the prayer after recovery if he could not face the qibla?
A) Because he was healthy otherwise.
B) Because conditions fall more readily than pillars, and pillars do not require repetition when impossible.
C) Because he prayed with full intention.
D) Because the qibla direction was unknown.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school applies the a fortiori argument to excuse conditions of prayer when the worshiper is unable, and no repetition is required upon recovery.

Praying with Gestures When No Pure Place Found
Ruling from al-Tajnīs on prayer with gestures when no pure place is available.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a person who cannot find pure ground for prayer and explain why repetition is wajib.

Core Idea
If the excuse is from the servant, the prayer with gestures must be repeated as qada.

This ruling from Abū Ḥanīfah addresses a traveler or someone in a situation where no pure place for prayer is available. The person prays with gestures (īmā’) to fulfill the obligation in time, but because the inability stemmed from his own circumstances, the prayer must be repeated later. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a deficiency from the servant’s side requires compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the initial ruling
The case: a person who has performed wudu but cannot find a pure place to pray. The initial ruling: he prays with gestures (īmā’) – i.e., bowing and prostrating by head movements.
The person is not excused from prayer entirely; he must still perform it in the best possible way.
2
Determine the need for repetition
After praying with gestures, he must repeat the prayer later (qaḍā’). The repetition is wajib because the excuse (lack of pure place) arose from the servant’s side – it is a human limitation, not a divinely imposed barrier.
Contrast with cases where the excuse is from Allah's side (e.g., rain), where repetition may not be required.
3
Understand the reason for repetition
The text says: 'He repeats because the excuse came from the servant.' This means the defect in the prayer (praying with gestures instead of normal performance) is attributed to the person's inability, so the full obligation is not discharged until the prayer is performed properly later.
This aligns with the principle that what is due cannot be fully fulfilled by a deficient substitute when the deficiency is avoidable in origin.
4
Confirm the hukm level
Praying with gestures when no pure place is found is wajib (obligatory) to avoid missing the time. Repetition is also wajib. The overall hukm for the initial act is valid but deficient, requiring compensation.
Had the person not prayed at all, he would be sinful; praying with gestures fulfills the obligation for the time but not completely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجنيس : قال أبو حنيفة في متوضىء لا يقدر على مكان طاهر وقد حضرت الصلاة صلى بالإيماء ثم يعيد ما صلى بالإيماء قضاء لحق الوقت بالتشبه وإنما يعيد لأن العذر جاء من قبل العبد. وقال يصلي الماشي وهو يمشي ولا السابح وهو يسبح في البحر ولا السائف وهو يضرب بالسيف لأن هذه الأفعال منافية للصلاة ولهذا شغل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن صلاته يوم الخندق لأجل قال : الغريق في البحر إذا حضرته الصلاة إن وجد ما يتعلق به أو كان ماهراً في السباحة بحيث يمكنه الصلاة بالإيماء من غير أن يحتاج فيه إلى عمل كثير افترض عليه أداء الصلاة لأنه قادر، ولو لم يجد ما يتعلق به ولم يكن ماهراً في السباحة يعذر بالتأخير إلى أن لأنه غير قادر على أداء الصلاة اهـ.
And in al-Tajnīs: Abū Ḥanīfah said concerning one who has performed wuḍū’ but cannot find a pure place, and the prayer time has come: he prays with gestures (īmā’), then he repeats what he prayed with gestures as a qaḍā’ (make-up) to fulfill the right of the time through similarity (tashabbuh); he repeats only because the excuse arose from the servant. And he said: A person walking prays while walking, but a swimmer does not pray while swimming, nor does a swordsman (al-sā’if) pray while striking with the sword, because these actions are incompatible with prayer. For this reason, the Prophet (ﷺ) was occupied from his prayer on the Day of the Trench due to... He said: A drowning person in the sea, when the prayer time comes – if he finds something to hold onto or is skilled in swimming such that he can pray with gestures without needing much action, then prayer is obligatory upon him because he is able. But if he finds nothing to hold onto and is not skilled in swimming, he is excused to delay until... because he is unable to perform the prayer. End quote.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from al-Tajnīs presents two distinct rulings by Abū Ḥanīfah. First: a person who has performed wuḍū’ but cannot find a pure place to pray – he prays with gestures (īmā’) and later repeats that prayer as qaḍā’. The reason for repetition is that the excuse (inability to find pure ground) stems from the servant’s side (i.e., a deficiency in his circumstances), so the prayer must be made up. Second: the ruling on praying while moving: a person walking may pray while walking (e.g., if in danger or necessity), but a swimmer or a swordsman engaged in fighting cannot pray because their actions are fundamentally incompatible with the nature of prayer (they involve extensive movement, loss of qibla, etc.). The drowning person is excused to delay prayer if he cannot perform it even with gestures; but if he can manage with gestures (by holding onto something or swimming skillfully), prayer is obligatory immediately.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā’): Gestures; making movements with the head for ruku and sujud instead of full bending, used when unable to prostrate normally.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Make-up; performing a missed or deficient act of worship later to fulfill the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must a person who prayed with gestures due to lack of pure ground repeat the prayer later?
A) Because the prayer was invalid.
B) Because the excuse came from the servant’s side, not from a divine cause.
C) Because he did not face the qibla.
D) Because he did not recite the Qur’an.

Key Takeaway
A prayer performed with gestures due to a servant-side excuse requires later repetition to fully discharge the obligation.

Walking, Swimming, and Fighting During Prayer
Rulings from al-Tajnīs on praying while moving and drowning person.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between walking prayer (valid) and swimming/fighting prayer (invalid) and state the ruling for a drowning person.

Core Idea
Walking while praying is allowed; swimming and fighting invalidate prayer due to excessive incompatible movement.

This portion of the passage from al-Tajnīs addresses three scenarios: a person walking while praying, a swimmer, and a swordsman (combatant). Abū Ḥanīfah rules that walking prayer is valid, but swimming and fighting render the prayer invalid because these involve actions fundamentally incompatible with the nature of prayer. Additionally, the drowning person is excused to delay prayer if unable, but must pray immediately if he can manage with gestures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases and their rulings
Case 1: A person walking (al-māshī) – he may pray while walking. Case 2: A swimmer (al-sābiḥ) – he may not pray while swimming. Case 3: A swordsman (al-sā’if) striking with the sword – he may not pray while fighting.
The key difference is the amount and nature of movement involved.
2
Analyze why walking is allowed but swimming and fighting are not
Walking does not necessarily conflict with the essence of prayer; a person can walk and still maintain the required postures and recitation. Swimming and fighting involve extensive, incompatible actions that break the state of prayer.
The text mentions 'because these actions are incompatible with prayer' and gives the example of the Prophet (ﷺ) being occupied from prayer on the Day of the Trench – indicating extreme circumstances.
3
Examine the drowning person's ruling
A drowning person (gharīq) in the sea: if he can hold onto something or is skilled enough to pray with gestures without excessive action, prayer is obligatory immediately. If he cannot, he is excused to delay.
This shows that inability to perform even gesture prayer excuses delay, but ability triggers immediate obligation.
4
Understand the principle behind these rulings
The Hanafi principle is that prayer requires a minimum of calm and focus (tuma’nīnah). Actions that disrupt this are permissible only in necessity (like walking) but not if they are inherently conflicting (like swimming or fighting).
Walking is a light action that can be done without invalidating the prayer if the walking is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجنيس : قال أبو حنيفة في متوضىء لا يقدر على مكان طاهر وقد حضرت الصلاة صلى بالإيماء ثم يعيد ما صلى بالإيماء قضاء لحق الوقت بالتشبه وإنما يعيد لأن العذر جاء من قبل العبد. وقال يصلي الماشي وهو يمشي ولا السابح وهو يسبح في البحر ولا السائف وهو يضرب بالسيف لأن هذه الأفعال منافية للصلاة ولهذا شغل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن صلاته يوم الخندق لأجل قال : الغريق في البحر إذا حضرته الصلاة إن وجد ما يتعلق به أو كان ماهراً في السباحة بحيث يمكنه الصلاة بالإيماء من غير أن يحتاج فيه إلى عمل كثير افترض عليه أداء الصلاة لأنه قادر، ولو لم يجد ما يتعلق به ولم يكن ماهراً في السباحة يعذر بالتأخير إلى أن لأنه غير قادر على أداء الصلاة اهـ.
And in al-Tajnīs: Abū Ḥanīfah said concerning one who has performed wuḍū’ but cannot find a pure place, and the prayer time has come: he prays with gestures (īmā’), then he repeats what he prayed with gestures as a qaḍā’ (make-up) to fulfill the right of the time through similarity (tashabbuh); he repeats only because the excuse arose from the servant. And he said: A person walking prays while walking, but a swimmer does not pray while swimming, nor does a swordsman (al-sā’if) pray while striking with the sword, because these actions are incompatible with prayer. For this reason, the Prophet (ﷺ) was occupied from his prayer on the Day of the Trench due to... But if he finds nothing to hold onto and is not skilled in swimming, he is excused to delay until... because he is unable to perform the prayer. End quote.

Commentary
This passage from al-Tajnīs presents two distinct rulings by Abū Ḥanīfah. First: a person who has performed wuḍū’ but cannot find a pure place to pray – he prays with gestures (īmā’) and later repeats that prayer as qaḍā’. Key Terms
الماشي (al-māshī): A person walking; in fiqh, one who prays while walking.
السابح (al-sābiḥ): A swimmer; one who swims during prayer.
السائف (al-sā’if): A swordsman; one engaged in combat with a sword.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following actions invalidates prayer?
A) Walking while praying
B) Swimming while praying
C) Both walking and swimming
D) Neither walking nor swimming

Key Takeaway
Walking prayer is valid in necessity; swimming and fighting invalidate prayer; drowning person must pray if able with gestures, otherwise delay.
